			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Peak oil</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg/220px-Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg/330px-Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg/440px-Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 1956 world oil production distribution, showing historical data and future production, proposed by <a href="/wiki/M._King_Hubbert" title="M. King Hubbert">M. King Hubbert</a> – it has a peak of 12.5 billion barrels per year in about the year 2000</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hubbert_Upper-Bound_Peak_1956.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Hubbert_Upper-Bound_Peak_1956.png/220px-Hubbert_Upper-Bound_Peak_1956.png" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Hubbert_Upper-Bound_Peak_1956.png/330px-Hubbert_Upper-Bound_Peak_1956.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Hubbert_Upper-Bound_Peak_1956.png/440px-Hubbert_Upper-Bound_Peak_1956.png 2x" data-file-width="1950" data-file-height="1425" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hubbert_Upper-Bound_Peak_1956.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hubbert's upper-bound prediction for US crude oil production (1956), and actual lower-48 states production through 2014</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Peak oil</b>, an event based on <a href="/wiki/M._King_Hubbert" title="M. King Hubbert">M. King Hubbert</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Hubbert_peak_theory" title="Hubbert peak theory">theory</a>, is the point in time when the maximum rate of <a href="/wiki/Extraction_of_petroleum" title="Extraction of petroleum">extraction of petroleum</a> is reached, after which it is expected to enter terminal decline.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Peak oil theory is based on the observed rise, peak, fall, and depletion of <a href="/wiki/Aggregate_data" title="Aggregate data">aggregate</a> production rate in <a href="/wiki/Oil_field" title="Oil field">oil fields</a> over time. It is often confused with <a href="/wiki/Oil_depletion" title="Oil depletion">oil depletion</a>; however, peak oil is the point of maximum production, while depletion refers to a period of falling reserves and supply.</p>
<p>Some observers, such as petroleum industry experts <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_S._Deffeyes" title="Kenneth S. Deffeyes">Kenneth S. Deffeyes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Simmons" title="Matthew Simmons">Matthew Simmons</a>, predict negative <a href="/wiki/World_economy" title="World economy">global economy</a> implications following a post-peak production decline and subsequent <a href="/wiki/Price_of_oil" title="Price of oil">oil price</a> increase because of the high dependence of most modern industrial <a href="/wiki/Transport" title="Transport">transport</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agricultural</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Industry" title="Industry">industrial</a> systems on the low cost and high availability of oil.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Predictions vary greatly as to what exactly these negative effects would be.</p>
<p>Oil production forecasts on which predictions of peak oil are based are often made within a range which includes optimistic (higher production) and pessimistic (lower production) scenarios. Optimistic<sup id="cite_ref-miller2013_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller2013-4">[4]</a></sup> estimations of peak production forecast the global decline will begin after 2020, and assume major investments in <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel" title="Alternative fuel">alternatives</a> will occur before a crisis, without requiring major changes in the lifestyle of heavily oil-consuming nations. Pessimistic predictions of future oil production made after 2007 stated either that the peak had already occurred,<sup id="cite_ref-deffeyes012007_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deffeyes012007-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ewg1007_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewg1007-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cohen102007_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cohen102007-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-alklett112009_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alklett112009-8">[8]</a></sup> that oil production was on the cusp of the peak, or that it would occur shortly.<sup id="cite_ref-koppelaar092006_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-koppelaar092006-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-owen032010_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-owen032010-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hubbert's original prediction that US peak oil would be in about 1970 seemed accurate for a time, as US average annual production peaked in 1970 at 9.6 million barrels per day.<sup id="cite_ref-EIAt_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIAt-11">[11]</a></sup> However, the successful application of massive <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a> to additional tight reservoirs caused US production to rebound, challenging the inevitability of post-peak decline for the US oil production.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-12">[12]</a></sup> In addition, Hubbert's original predictions for world peak oil production proved premature.<sup id="cite_ref-miller2013_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller2013-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modeling_global_oil_production">Modeling global oil production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Modeling global oil production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hubbert_peak_theory" title="Hubbert peak theory">Hubbert peak theory</a></div>
<p>The idea that the rate of oil production would peak and irreversibly decline is an old one. In 1919, David White, chief geologist of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a>, wrote of US petroleum: "...&#160;the peak of production will soon be passed, possibly within 3 years."<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In 1953, Eugene Ayers, a researcher for <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Oil" title="Gulf Oil">Gulf Oil</a>, projected that if US ultimate recoverable oil reserves were 100 billion barrels, then production in the US would peak no later than 1960. If ultimate recoverable were to be as high as 200 billion barrels, which he warned was wishful thinking, US peak production would come no later than 1970. Likewise for the world, he projected a peak somewhere between 1985 (one trillion barrels ultimate recoverable) and 2000 (two trillion barrels recoverable). Ayers made his projections without a mathematical model. He wrote: "But if the curve is made to look reasonable, it is quite possible to adapt mathematical expressions to it and to determine, in this way, the peak dates corresponding to various ultimate recoverable reserve numbers"<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>By observing past discoveries and production levels, and predicting future discovery trends, the geoscientist M. King Hubbert used statistical modelling in 1956 to accurately<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> predict that United States oil production would peak between 1965 and 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-mkinghubbert1956_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mkinghubbert1956-16">[16]</a></sup> Hubbert used a semi-<a href="/wiki/Logistic_distribution" title="Logistic distribution">logistical</a> curved model (sometimes incorrectly compared to a <a href="/wiki/Normal_distribution" title="Normal distribution">normal distribution</a>). He assumed the production rate of a limited resource would follow a roughly symmetrical distribution. Depending on the limits of exploitability and market pressures, the rise or decline of resource production over time might be sharper or more stable, appear more linear or curved.<sup id="cite_ref-Brandt2007_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brandt2007-17">[17]</a></sup> That model and its variants are now called <a href="/wiki/Hubbert_peak_theory" title="Hubbert peak theory">Hubbert peak theory</a>; they have been used to describe and predict the peak and decline of production from regions, countries, and multinational areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Brandt2007_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brandt2007-17">[17]</a></sup> The same theory has also been applied to other limited-resource production.</p>
<p>In a 2006 analysis of Hubbert theory, it was noted that uncertainty in real world oil production amounts and confusion in definitions increases the uncertainty in general of production predictions. By comparing the fit of various other models, it was found that Hubbert's methods yielded the closest fit over all, but that none of the models were very accurate.<sup id="cite_ref-Brandt2007_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brandt2007-17">[17]</a></sup> In 1956 Hubbert himself recommended using "a family of possible production curves" when predicting a production peak and decline curve.<sup id="cite_ref-mkinghubbert1956_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mkinghubbert1956-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>More recently, the term "peak oil" was popularized by <a href="/wiki/Colin_Campbell_(geologist)" title="Colin Campbell (geologist)">Colin Campbell</a> and Kjell Aleklett in 2002 when they helped form the Association for the Study of Peak Oil and Gas (ASPO).<sup id="cite_ref-wakeford2012_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wakeford2012-18">[18]</a></sup> In his publications, Hubbert used the term "peak production rate" and "peak in the rate of discoveries".<sup id="cite_ref-mkinghubbert1956_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mkinghubbert1956-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demand">Demand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Demand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Global_oil_consumption_1980-2013.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Global_oil_consumption_1980-2013.png/220px-Global_oil_consumption_1980-2013.png" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Global_oil_consumption_1980-2013.png/330px-Global_oil_consumption_1980-2013.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Global_oil_consumption_1980-2013.png/440px-Global_oil_consumption_1980-2013.png 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="529" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Global_oil_consumption_1980-2013.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Global consumption of oil 1980–2013 (Energy Information Administration)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">demand</a> side of peak oil over time is concerned with the total quantity of oil that the global market would choose to consume at various possible market prices and how this entire listing of quantities at various prices would evolve over time. Global demand for crude oil grew an average of 1.76% per year from 1994 to 2006, with a high growth of 3.4% in 2003–2004. After reaching a high of 85.6 million barrels (13,610,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) per day in 2007, world consumption decreased in both 2008 and 2009 by a total of 1.8%, despite fuel costs plummeting in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Despite this lull, world quantity-demanded for oil is projected to increase 21% over 2007 levels by 2030 (104 million barrels per day (16.5<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>6</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d) from 86 million barrels (13.7<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>6</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>)), or about 0.8% average annual growth, due in large part to increases in demand from the transportation sector.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc062006_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc062006-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-eia2007ieo_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eia2007ieo-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> According to projections by the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> (IEA) in 2013, growth in global oil demand will be significantly outpaced by growth in production capacity over the next 5 years.<sup id="cite_ref-ieamtomr2013_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ieamtomr2013-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-time051513_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time051513-24">[24]</a></sup> Developments in late 2014–2015 have seen an oversupply of global markets leading to a significant drop in the price of oil.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/World_energy_resources_and_consumption" class="mw-redirect" title="World energy resources and consumption">Energy demand</a> is distributed amongst four broad sectors: transportation, <a href="/wiki/Domestic_Energy_Consumption" class="mw-redirect" title="Domestic Energy Consumption">residential</a>, commercial, and industrial.<sup id="cite_ref-eia2006aer_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eia2006aer-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-eia2007goc_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eia2007goc-27">[27]</a></sup> In terms of oil use, transportation is the largest sector and the one that has seen the largest growth in demand in recent decades. This growth has largely come from new demand for personal-use vehicles powered by <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wood082004_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wood082004-28">[28]</a></sup> This sector also has the highest consumption rates, accounting for approximately 71% of the oil used in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> and 55% of oil use worldwide as documented in the <a href="/wiki/Hirsch_report" title="Hirsch report">Hirsch report</a>. Transportation is therefore of particular interest to those seeking to mitigate the effects of peak oil.</p>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:752px;"><a href="/wiki/File:OilConsumptionpercapita.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/OilConsumptionpercapita.png/750px-OilConsumptionpercapita.png" width="750" height="347" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/OilConsumptionpercapita.png/1125px-OilConsumptionpercapita.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/OilConsumptionpercapita.png 2x" data-file-width="1357" data-file-height="628" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:OilConsumptionpercapita.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Oil consumption per capita (darker colors represent more consumption, gray represents no data) <i>(source: see file description)</i>
<table style="width: 100%; font-size: 1em;">
<tr>
<td valign="top"><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#800000; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> &gt; 0.07</span><br />
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#a52a2a; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> 0.07 - 0.05</span><br />
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#ff0000; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> 0.05 - 0.035</span><br />
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#fa8072; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> 0.035 - 0.025</span><br />
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#f08080; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> 0.025 - 0.02</span><br /></td>
<td valign="top"><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#ffb6c1; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> 0.02 - 0.015</span><br />
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#ffebda; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> 0.015 - 0.01</span><br />
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#ffebcf; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> 0.01 - 0.005</span><br />
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#fff8dc; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> 0.005 - 0.0015</span><br />
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#e6e6fa; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span> &lt; 0.0015</span><br /></td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although demand growth is highest in the <a href="/wiki/Developing_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Developing world">developing world</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-eiaoilconsumption_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eiaoilconsumption-30">[30]</a></sup> the United States is the world's largest consumer of petroleum. Between 1995 and 2005, US consumption grew from 17,700,000 barrels per day (2,810,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d) to 20,700,000 barrels per day (3,290,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d), a 3,000,000 barrels per day (480,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d) increase. China, by comparison, increased consumption from 3,400,000 barrels per day (540,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d) to 7,000,000 barrels per day (1,100,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d), an increase of 3,600,000 barrels per day (570,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d), in the same time frame.<sup id="cite_ref-BP2008_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP2008-31">[31]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">Energy Information Administration</a> (EIA) stated that gasoline usage in the United States may have peaked in 2007, in part because of increasing interest in and mandates for use of biofuels and energy efficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>As countries <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">develop</a>, industry and higher <a href="/wiki/Living_standards" class="mw-redirect" title="Living standards">living standards</a> drive up energy use, oil usage being a major component. Thriving economies, such as <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Republic of China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, are quickly becoming large oil consumers.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc070508_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc070508-34">[34]</a></sup> For example, China surpassed the United States as the world's largest crude oil importer in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Oil consumption growth is expected to continue; however, not at previous rates, as China's economic growth is predicted to decrease from the high rates of the early part of the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> India's oil imports are expected to more than triple from 2005 levels by 2020, rising to 5 million barrels per day (790×103 m<sup>3</sup>/d).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Population">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:World_population_history.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/World_population_history.svg/220px-World_population_history.svg.png" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/World_population_history.svg/330px-World_population_history.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/World_population_history.svg/440px-World_population_history.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="320" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:World_population_history.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
World population</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another significant factor affecting petroleum demand has been human <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a>. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">United States Census Bureau</a> predicts that <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> in 2030 will be almost double that of 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-censusworldpop_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censusworldpop-38">[38]</a></sup> Oil production per capita peaked in 1979 at 5.5 barrels/year but then declined to fluctuate around 4.5 barrels/year since.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In this regard, the decreasing population growth rate since the 1970s has somewhat ameliorated the per capita decline.<sup id="cite_ref-censusworldpop_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censusworldpop-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_growth">Economic growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Economic growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some analysts argue that the cost of oil has a profound effect on economic growth due to its pivotal role in the extraction of resources and the processing, manufacturing, and transportation of goods.<sup id="cite_ref-How_Economic_Growth_Fails_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_Economic_Growth_Fails-40">[40]</a></sup> As the industrial effort to extract new unconventional oil sources increases, this has a compounding negative effect on all sectors of the economy, leading to economic stagnation or even eventual contraction. Such a scenario would result in an inability for national economies to pay high oil prices, leading to declining demand and a price collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Supply">Supply</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Supply">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Global_liquids_and_US_tight_oil.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Global_liquids_and_US_tight_oil.png/220px-Global_liquids_and_US_tight_oil.png" width="220" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Global_liquids_and_US_tight_oil.png/330px-Global_liquids_and_US_tight_oil.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Global_liquids_and_US_tight_oil.png/440px-Global_liquids_and_US_tight_oil.png 2x" data-file-width="1128" data-file-height="655" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Global_liquids_and_US_tight_oil.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Global liquids production 2000-2015, indicating the component of US tight oil (Energy Information Administration)</div>
</div>
</div>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Our analysis suggests there are ample physical oil and liquid fuel resources for the foreseeable future. However, the rate at which new supplies can be developed and the break-even prices for those new supplies are changing.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defining_sources_of_oil">Defining sources of oil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Defining sources of oil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Oil may come from conventional or unconventional sources. The terms are not strictly defined, and vary within the literature as definitions based on new technologies tend to change over time.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> As a result, different oil forecasting studies have included different classes of liquid fuels. Some use the terms "conventional" oil for what is included in the model, and "unconventional" oil for classes excluded.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1956, Hubbert confined his peak oil prediction to that crude oil "producible by methods now in use."<sup id="cite_ref-mkinghubbert1956_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mkinghubbert1956-16">[16]</a></sup> By 1962, however, his analyses included future improvements in exploration and production.<sup id="cite_ref-M._King_Hubbert_1962_p.73-75_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M._King_Hubbert_1962_p.73-75-45">[45]</a></sup> All of Hubbert's analyses of peak oil specifically excluded oil manufactured from oil shale or mined from oil sands. A 2013 study predicting an early peak excluded deepwater oil, tight oil, oil with API gravity less than 17.5, and oil close to the poles, such as that on the North Slope of Alaska, all of which it defined as non-conventional.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Some commonly used definitions for conventional and unconventional oil are detailed below.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conventional_sources">Conventional sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Conventional sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Conventional oil is extracted on land and offshore using standard techniques,<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and can be categorized as light, medium, heavy, or extra heavy in grade. The exact definitions of these grades vary depending on the region from which the oil came.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Light oil flows naturally to the surface or can be extracted by simply pumping it out of the ground. Heavy refers to oil that has higher density and therefore lower <a href="/wiki/API_gravity" title="API gravity">API gravity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> It does not flow easily, and its consistency is similar to that of molasses. While some of it can be produced using conventional techniques, recovery rates are better using unconventional methods.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unconventional_sources">Unconventional sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Unconventional sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Oil currently considered unconventional is derived from multiple sources.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tight_oil" title="Tight oil">Tight oil</a> is extracted from deposits of low-permeability rock, sometimes shale deposits but often other rock types, using <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a>, or "fracking."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> It is often confused with shale oil, which is oil manufactured from the kerogen contained in an oil shale (see below), Production of tight oil has led to a resurgence of US production in recent years. However, tight oil production peaked in 2015 and is not expected to increase again until there is a significant oil price recovery.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lower_48_production_2012-17.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Lower_48_production_2012-17.png/220px-Lower_48_production_2012-17.png" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Lower_48_production_2012-17.png/330px-Lower_48_production_2012-17.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Lower_48_production_2012-17.png/440px-Lower_48_production_2012-17.png 2x" data-file-width="575" data-file-height="287" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lower_48_production_2012-17.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
US Lower 48 oil production from 2012 and anticipated decline in production to the end of 2017, with rig count (Energy Information Administration)</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oil_shale" title="Oil shale">Oil shale</a> is a common term for sedimentary rock such as <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">shale</a> or <a href="/wiki/Marl" title="Marl">marl</a>, containing <a href="/wiki/Kerogen" title="Kerogen">kerogen</a>, a waxy oil precursor that has not yet been transformed into crude oil by the high pressures and temperatures caused by deep burial. The term "oil shale" is somewhat confusing, because what is referred to in the U.S. as "oil shale" is not really oil and the rock it is found in is generally not shale.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Since it is close to the surface rather than buried deep in the earth, the shale or marl is typically mined, crushed, and <a href="/wiki/Retort" title="Retort">retorted</a>, producing synthetic oil from the kerogen.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Energy_returned_on_energy_invested" title="Energy returned on energy invested">net energy yield</a> is much lower than conventional oil, so much so that estimates of the net energy yield of shale discoveries are considered extremely unreliable.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oil_sands" title="Oil sands">Oil sands</a> are unconsolidated <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> deposits containing large amounts of very viscous crude <a href="/wiki/Bitumen" class="mw-redirect" title="Bitumen">bitumen</a> or extra-<a href="/wiki/Heavy_crude_oil" title="Heavy crude oil">heavy crude oil</a> that can be recovered by <a href="/wiki/Surface_mining" title="Surface mining">surface mining</a> or by <a href="/wiki/In-situ" class="mw-redirect" title="In-situ">in-situ</a> oil wells using <a href="/wiki/Steam_injection_(oil_industry)" title="Steam injection (oil industry)">steam injection</a> or other techniques. It can be liquefied by <a href="/wiki/Upgrader" title="Upgrader">upgrading</a>, blending with <a href="/wiki/Diluent" title="Diluent">diluent</a>, or by heating; and then processed by a conventional <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">oil refinery</a>. The recovery process requires advanced <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a> but is more efficient than that of oil shale. The reason is that, unlike U.S. "oil shale", Canadian oil sands actually contain oil, and the sandstones they are found in are much easier to produce oil from than shale or marl. In the <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">U.S. dialect of English</a>, these formations are often called "tar sands", but the material found in them is not tar but an extra-heavy and viscous form of oil technically known as <a href="/wiki/Bitumen" class="mw-redirect" title="Bitumen">bitumen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg/220px-US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg.png" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg/330px-US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg/440px-US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="540" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Crude_Oil_Production_and_Imports.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
United States crude oil production exceeds imports for the first time since the early 1990s</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Coal_liquefaction" title="Coal liquefaction">Coal liquefaction</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gas_to_liquids" title="Gas to liquids">gas to liquids</a> product are liquid hydrocarbons that are <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fuel" title="Synthetic fuel">synthesised</a> from the conversion of coal or natural gas by the <a href="/wiki/Fischer-Tropsch_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Fischer-Tropsch process">Fischer-Tropsch process</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bergius_process" title="Bergius process">Bergius process</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Karrick_process" title="Karrick process">Karrick process</a>. Currently, two companies <a href="/wiki/SASOL" class="mw-redirect" title="SASOL">SASOL</a> and <a href="/wiki/Royal_Dutch_Shell" title="Royal Dutch Shell">Shell</a>, have synthetic oil technology proven to work on a commercial scale. Sasol's primary business is based on CTL (coal-to-liquid) and GTL (natural gas-to-liquid) technology, producing US$4.40 billion in revenues (FY2009). Shell has used these processes to recycle waste <a href="/wiki/Gas_flare" title="Gas flare">flare gas</a> (usually burnt off at oil wells and refineries) into usable synthetic oil. However, for CTL there may be insufficient coal reserves to supply global needs for both liquid fuels and electric power generation.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></li>
<li>Minor sources include <a href="/wiki/Thermal_depolymerization" title="Thermal depolymerization">thermal depolymerization</a>, as discussed in a 2003 article in <a href="/wiki/Discover_(magazine)" title="Discover (magazine)"><i>Discover</i> magazine</a>, that could be used to manufacture oil indefinitely, out of garbage, sewage, and agricultural waste. The article claimed that the cost of the process was $15 per barrel.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> A follow-up article in 2006 stated that the cost was actually $80 per barrel, because the feedstock that had previously been considered as hazardous waste now had market value.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> A 2008 news bulletin published by <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_Laboratory" class="mw-redirect" title="Los Alamos Laboratory">Los Alamos Laboratory</a> proposed that <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">hydrogen</a> (possibly produced using hot fluid from nuclear reactors to <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">split water</a> into hydrogen and oxygen) in combination with <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage#Sequestration" title="Carbon capture and storage">sequestered CO<sub>2</sub></a> could be used to produce <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> (CH<sub>3</sub>OH), which could then be converted into gasoline.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discoveries">Discoveries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Discoveries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>All the easy oil and gas in the world has pretty much been found. Now comes the harder work in finding and producing oil from more challenging environments and work areas.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— William J. Cummings, Exxon-Mobil company spokesman, December 2005<sup id="cite_ref-bostonglobe122005_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bostonglobe122005-62">[62]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is pretty clear that there is not much chance of finding any significant quantity of new cheap oil. Any new or unconventional oil is going to be expensive.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Lord Ron Oxburgh, a former chairman of Shell, October 2008<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:World_crude_discovery_production_U-2200Gb_LaherrereMar2015.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/World_crude_discovery_production_U-2200Gb_LaherrereMar2015.jpg/220px-World_crude_discovery_production_U-2200Gb_LaherrereMar2015.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/World_crude_discovery_production_U-2200Gb_LaherrereMar2015.jpg/330px-World_crude_discovery_production_U-2200Gb_LaherrereMar2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/World_crude_discovery_production_U-2200Gb_LaherrereMar2015.jpg/440px-World_crude_discovery_production_U-2200Gb_LaherrereMar2015.jpg 2x" data-file-width="875" data-file-height="653" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:World_crude_discovery_production_U-2200Gb_LaherrereMar2015.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
World oil discoveries peaked in the 1960s</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The peak of world oilfield discoveries occurred in the 1960s<sup id="cite_ref-campbell1222000_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-campbell1222000-64">[64]</a></sup> at around 55 billion barrels (8.7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>)(Gb)/year.<sup id="cite_ref-longwell2002_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-longwell2002-65">[65]</a></sup> According to the Association for the Study of Peak Oil and Gas (ASPO), the rate of discovery has been falling steadily since. Less than 10 Gb/yr of oil were discovered each year between 2002 and 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> According to a 2010 Reuters article, the annual rate of discovery of new fields has remained remarkably constant at 15–20 Gb/yr.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Revisions_to_US_Oil_Reserves_2011.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Revisions_to_US_Oil_Reserves_2011.png/220px-Revisions_to_US_Oil_Reserves_2011.png" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Revisions_to_US_Oil_Reserves_2011.png/330px-Revisions_to_US_Oil_Reserves_2011.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Revisions_to_US_Oil_Reserves_2011.png/440px-Revisions_to_US_Oil_Reserves_2011.png 2x" data-file-width="630" data-file-height="511" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Revisions_to_US_Oil_Reserves_2011.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Although US proved oil reserves grew by 3.8 billion barrels in 2011, even after deducting 2.07 billion barrels of production, only 8 percent of the 5.84 billion barrels of the newly booked oil was because of new field discoveries (<a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">U.S. EIA</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>But despite the fall-off in new field discoveries, and record-high production rates, the reported proved reserves of crude oil remaining in the ground in 2014, which totaled 1,490 billion barrels, not counting Canadian heavy oil sands, were more than quadruple the 1965 proved reserves of 354 billion barrels.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> A researcher for the <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">U.S. Energy Information Administration</a> has pointed out that after the first wave of discoveries in an area, most oil and natural gas reserve growth comes not from discoveries of new fields, but from extensions and additional gas found within existing fields.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>A report by the UK Energy Research Centre noted that "discovery" is often used ambiguously, and explained the seeming contradiction between falling discovery rates since the 1960s and increasing reserves by the phenomenon of reserve growth. The report noted that increased reserves within a field may be discovered or developed by new technology years or decades after the original discovery. But because of the practice of "backdating," any new reserves within a field, even those to be discovered decades after the field discovery, are attributed to the year of initial field discovery, creating an illusion that discovery is not keeping pace with production.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reserves">Reserves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Reserves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Oil_reserves" title="Oil reserves">Oil reserves</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_oil_fields" class="mw-redirect" title="List of largest oil fields">List of largest oil fields</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_Reserves.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Oil_Reserves.png/350px-Oil_Reserves.png" width="350" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Oil_Reserves.png/525px-Oil_Reserves.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Oil_Reserves.png/700px-Oil_Reserves.png 2x" data-file-width="1799" data-file-height="819" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_Reserves.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Proven <a href="/wiki/Oil_reserves" title="Oil reserves">oil reserves</a>, 2013</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Total possible conventional crude <a href="/wiki/Oil_reserves" title="Oil reserves">oil reserves</a> include crude oil with 90% certainty of being technically able to be produced from reservoirs (through a wellbore using primary, secondary, improved, enhanced, or tertiary methods); all crude with a 50% probability of being produced in the future (probable); and discovered reserves that have a 10% possibility of being produced in the future (possible). Reserve estimates based on these are referred to as 1P, proven (at least 90% probability); 2P, proven and probable (at least 50% probability); and 3P, proven, probable and possible (at least 10% probability), respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> This does not include liquids extracted from mined solids or gasses (<a href="/wiki/Oil_sand" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil sand">oil sands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oil_shale" title="Oil shale">oil shale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gas_to_liquids" title="Gas to liquids">gas-to-liquid processes</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Coal_liquefaction" title="Coal liquefaction">coal-to-liquid processes</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-iht072007_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iht072007-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hubbert's 1956 peak projection for the United States depended on geological estimates of ultimate recoverable oil resources, but starting in his 1962 publication, he concluded that ultimate oil recovery was an output of his mathematical analysis, rather than an assumption. He regarded his peak oil calculation as independent of reserve estimates.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many current 2P calculations predict reserves to be between 1150 and 1350 Gb, but some authors have written that because of misinformation, withheld information, and misleading reserve calculations, 2P reserves are likely nearer to 850–900 Gb.<sup id="cite_ref-ewg1007_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewg1007-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-owen032010_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-owen032010-10">[10]</a></sup> The Energy Watch Group wrote that actual reserves peaked in 1980, when production first surpassed new discoveries, that apparent increases in reserves since then are illusory, and concluded (in 2007): "Probably the world oil production has peaked already, but we cannot be sure yet."<sup id="cite_ref-ewg1007_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewg1007-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Concerns_over_stated_reserves">Concerns over stated reserves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Concerns over stated reserves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[World] reserves are confused and in fact inflated. Many of the so-called reserves are in fact resources. They're not delineated, they're not accessible, they're not available for production.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Sadad_Ibrahim_Al_Husseini" title="Sadad Ibrahim Al Husseini">Sadad Al Husseini</a>, former VP of <a href="/wiki/Aramco" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramco">Aramco</a>, presentation to the Oil and Money conference, October 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-cohen102007_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cohen102007-7">[7]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sadad_Ibrahim_Al_Husseini" title="Sadad Ibrahim Al Husseini">Sadad Al Husseini</a> estimated that 300 billion barrels (48<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>9</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of the world's 1,200 billion barrels (190<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>9</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of proven reserves should be recategorized as speculative resources.<sup id="cite_ref-cohen102007_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cohen102007-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:OPEC_declared_reserves_1980-now_EIA.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/OPEC_declared_reserves_1980-now_EIA.svg/400px-OPEC_declared_reserves_1980-now_EIA.svg.png" width="400" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/OPEC_declared_reserves_1980-now_EIA.svg/600px-OPEC_declared_reserves_1980-now_EIA.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/OPEC_declared_reserves_1980-now_EIA.svg/800px-OPEC_declared_reserves_1980-now_EIA.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1050" data-file-height="700" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:OPEC_declared_reserves_1980-now_EIA.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Graph of OPEC reported reserves showing jumps in stated reserves without associated discoveries, as well as the lack of depletion despite yearly production</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One difficulty in forecasting the date of peak oil is the opacity surrounding the oil reserves classified as "proven". In many major producing countries, the majority of reserves claims have not been subject to outside audit or examination.<sup id="cite_ref-bostonglobe122005_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bostonglobe122005-62">[62]</a></sup> Many worrying signs concerning the depletion of proven reserves have emerged in recent years.<sup id="cite_ref-boxell102004_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boxell102004-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gerth022004_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gerth022004-77">[77]</a></sup> This was best exemplified by the 2004 scandal surrounding the "evaporation" of 20% of <a href="/wiki/Shell_oil_company" class="mw-redirect" title="Shell oil company">Shell</a>'s reserves.<sup id="cite_ref-shellblewrep_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shellblewrep-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>For the most part, proven reserves are stated by the oil companies, the producer states and the consumer states. All three have reasons to overstate their proven reserves: oil companies may look to increase their potential worth; producer countries gain a stronger <a href="/wiki/International_relations" title="International relations">international stature</a>; and governments of consumer countries may seek a means to foster sentiments of <a href="/wiki/Security" title="Security">security</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">stability</a> within their <a href="/wiki/Economy" title="Economy">economies</a> and among consumers.</p>
<p>Major discrepancies arise from accuracy issues with the self-reported numbers from the <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_the_Petroleum_Exporting_Countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries">Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries</a> (OPEC). Besides the possibility that these nations have overstated their reserves for political reasons (during periods of no substantial discoveries), over 70 nations also follow a practice of not reducing their reserves to account for yearly production. Analysts have suggested that OPEC member nations have economic incentives to exaggerate their reserves, as the OPEC quota system allows greater output for countries with greater reserves.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt08212005_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt08212005-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, for example, was reported in the January 2006 issue of <i>Petroleum Intelligence Weekly</i> to have only 48 billion barrels (7.6<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>9</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) in reserve, of which only 24 were fully proven. This report was based on the leak of a confidential document from Kuwait and has not been formally denied by the Kuwaiti authorities. This leaked document is from 2001,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> but excludes revisions or discoveries made since then. Additionally, the reported 1.5 billion barrels (240<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>6</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of <a href="/wiki/Kuwaiti_oil_fires" title="Kuwaiti oil fires">oil burned off</a> by Iraqi soldiers in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">First Persian Gulf War</a><sup id="cite_ref-TEDkuwait_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TEDkuwait-81">[81]</a></sup> are conspicuously missing from Kuwait's figures.</p>
<p>On the other hand, investigative journalist <a href="/wiki/Greg_Palast" title="Greg Palast">Greg Palast</a> argues that oil companies have an interest in making oil look more rare than it is, to justify higher prices.<sup id="cite_ref-gnn052006_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gnn052006-82">[82]</a></sup> This view is contested by ecological journalist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Heinberg" title="Richard Heinberg">Richard Heinberg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Heinberg_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heinberg-83">[83]</a></sup> Other analysts argue that oil producing countries understate the extent of their reserves to drive up the price.<sup id="cite_ref-nationalreview122003_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nationalreview122003-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>The EUR reported by the 2000 USGS survey of 2,300 billion barrels (370<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>9</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) has been criticized for assuming a discovery trend over the next twenty years that would reverse the observed trend of the past 40 years. Their 95% confidence EUR of 2,300 billion barrels (370<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>9</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) assumed that discovery levels would stay steady, despite the fact that new-field discovery rates have declined since the 1960s. That trend of falling discoveries has continued in the ten years since the USGS made their assumption. The 2000 USGS is also criticized for other assumptions, as well as assuming 2030 production rates inconsistent with projected reserves.<sup id="cite_ref-ewg1007_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewg1007-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reserves_of_unconventional_oil">Reserves of unconventional oil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Reserves of unconventional oil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Unconventional_oil" title="Unconventional oil">Unconventional oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heavy_crude_oil" title="Heavy crude oil">Heavy crude oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oil_sands" title="Oil sands">Oil sands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oil_shale" title="Oil shale">Oil shale</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fischer-Tropsch_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Fischer-Tropsch process">Fischer-Tropsch process</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Syncrude_mildred_lake_plant.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Syncrude_mildred_lake_plant.jpg/220px-Syncrude_mildred_lake_plant.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Syncrude_mildred_lake_plant.jpg/330px-Syncrude_mildred_lake_plant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Syncrude_mildred_lake_plant.jpg/440px-Syncrude_mildred_lake_plant.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Syncrude_mildred_lake_plant.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Syncrude's Mildred Lake mine site and plant near <a href="/wiki/Fort_McMurray" title="Fort McMurray">Fort McMurray</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As conventional oil becomes less available, it can be replaced with production of liquids from unconventional sources such as <a href="/wiki/Tight_oil" title="Tight oil">tight oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oil_sands" title="Oil sands">oil sands</a>, ultra-heavy oils, <a href="/wiki/Gas-to-liquid" class="mw-redirect" title="Gas-to-liquid">gas-to-liquid</a> technologies, <a href="/wiki/Coal-to-liquid" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal-to-liquid">coal-to-liquid</a> technologies, biofuel technologies, and <a href="/wiki/Shale_oil" title="Shale oil">shale oil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> In the 2007 and subsequent International Energy Outlook editions, the word "Oil" was replaced with "Liquids" in the chart of <a href="/wiki/World_energy_resources_and_consumption" class="mw-redirect" title="World energy resources and consumption">world energy consumption</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> In 2009 biofuels was included in "Liquids" instead of in "Renewables".<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The inclusion of natural gas liquids, a bi-product of natural gas extraction, in "Liquids" has been criticized as it is mostly a chemical feedstock which is generally not used as transport fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Texas_Oil_Production_1935-2012.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Texas_Oil_Production_1935-2012.png/220px-Texas_Oil_Production_1935-2012.png" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Texas_Oil_Production_1935-2012.png/330px-Texas_Oil_Production_1935-2012.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Texas_Oil_Production_1935-2012.png/440px-Texas_Oil_Production_1935-2012.png 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1375" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Texas_Oil_Production_1935-2012.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Texas oil production declined since peaking in 1972 but has recently had a resurgence due to tight oil production</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Reserve estimates are based on the oil price. Hence, unconventional sources such as heavy crude oil, oil sands, and oil shale may be included as new techniques reduce the cost of extraction.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> With rule changes by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">SEC</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> oil companies can now book them as proven reserves after opening a <a href="/wiki/Strip_mine" class="mw-redirect" title="Strip mine">strip mine</a> or thermal facility for <a href="/wiki/Resource_extraction" class="mw-redirect" title="Resource extraction">extraction</a>. These unconventional sources are more labor and resource intensive to produce, however, requiring extra energy to refine, resulting in higher production costs and up to three times more <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions per barrel (or barrel equivalent) on a "well to tank" basis or 10 to 45% more on a "well to wheels" basis, which includes the carbon emitted from combustion of the final product.<sup id="cite_ref-MJ_Herald_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MJ_Herald-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>While the energy used, resources needed, and environmental effects of extracting unconventional sources have traditionally been prohibitively high, major <a href="/wiki/Unconventional_oil" title="Unconventional oil">unconventional oil</a> sources being considered for large-scale production are the extra heavy oil in the <a href="/wiki/Orinoco_Belt" title="Orinoco Belt">Orinoco Belt</a> of <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Athabasca_Oil_Sands" class="mw-redirect" title="Athabasca Oil Sands">Athabasca Oil Sands</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Canadian_Sedimentary_Basin" title="Western Canadian Sedimentary Basin">Western Canadian Sedimentary Basin</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> and the oil shale of the <a href="/wiki/Green_River_Formation" title="Green River Formation">Green River Formation</a> in <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a> in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-dyni_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dyni-96">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fossilenergy_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fossilenergy-97">[97]</a></sup> Energy companies such as <a href="/wiki/Syncrude" title="Syncrude">Syncrude</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suncor" class="mw-redirect" title="Suncor">Suncor</a> have been extracting bitumen for decades but production has increased greatly in recent years with the development of <a href="/wiki/Steam_Assisted_Gravity_Drainage" class="mw-redirect" title="Steam Assisted Gravity Drainage">Steam Assisted Gravity Drainage</a> and other extraction technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Chuck Masters of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">USGS</a> estimates that, "Taken together, these resource occurrences, in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">Western Hemisphere</a>, are approximately equal to the Identified Reserves of conventional crude oil accredited to the Middle East."<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Authorities familiar with the resources believe that the world's ultimate reserves of unconventional oil are several times as large as those of conventional oil and will be highly profitable for companies as a result of higher prices in the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> In October 2009, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">USGS</a> updated the Orinoco tar sands (Venezuela) recoverable "mean value" to 513 billion barrels (8.16<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>), with a 90% chance of being within the range of 380-652 billion barrels (103.7<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>9</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>), making this area "one of the world's largest recoverable oil accumulations".<sup id="cite_ref-USGS_Venezuela_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS_Venezuela-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Total_World_Oil_Reserves.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Total_World_Oil_Reserves.PNG/350px-Total_World_Oil_Reserves.PNG" width="350" height="278" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Total_World_Oil_Reserves.PNG/525px-Total_World_Oil_Reserves.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Total_World_Oil_Reserves.PNG/700px-Total_World_Oil_Reserves.PNG 2x" data-file-width="734" data-file-height="583" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Total_World_Oil_Reserves.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Unconventional resources are much larger than conventional ones<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite the large quantities of oil available in non-conventional sources, Matthew Simmons argued in 2005 that limitations on production prevent them from becoming an effective substitute for conventional crude oil. Simmons stated "these are high energy intensity projects that can never reach high volumes" to offset significant losses from other sources.<sup id="cite_ref-wood052005_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wood052005-103">[103]</a></sup> Another study claims that even under highly optimistic assumptions, "Canada's oil sands will not prevent peak oil," although production could reach 5,000,000&#160;bbl/d (790,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d) by 2030 in a "crash program" development effort.<sup id="cite_ref-SoumlEtAl2007_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SoumlEtAl2007-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Moreover, oil extracted from these sources typically contains contaminants such as <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">heavy metals</a> that are energy-intensive to extract and can leave <a href="/wiki/Tailings" title="Tailings">tailings</a>, ponds containing hydrocarbon sludge, in some cases.<sup id="cite_ref-MJ_Herald_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MJ_Herald-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The same applies to much of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>'s undeveloped conventional oil reserves, much of which is heavy, viscous, and contaminated with sulfur and metals to the point of being unusable.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Oil_price_increases_since_2003" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil price increases since 2003">high oil prices</a> make these sources more financially appealing.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt08212005_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt08212005-79">[79]</a></sup> A study by Wood Mackenzie suggests that by the early 2020s all the world's extra oil supply is likely to come from unconventional sources.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">Petroleum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Means_of_production" title="Means of production">Means of production</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Extraction_of_petroleum" title="Extraction of petroleum">Extraction of petroleum</a></div>
<p>The point in time when peak global oil production occurs defines peak oil. Some adherents of 'peak oil' believe that production capacity will remain the main limitation of supply, and that when production decreases, it will be the main <a href="/wiki/Bottleneck_(logistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bottleneck (logistics)">bottleneck</a> to the petroleum <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">supply/demand</a> equation.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Others believe that the increasing industrial effort to extract oil will have a negative effect on global economic growth, leading to demand contraction and a price collapse,<sup id="cite_ref-How_Economic_Growth_Fails_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_Economic_Growth_Fails-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> thereby causing production decline as some unconventional sources become uneconomical. Yet others believe that the peak may be to some extent led by declining demand as new technologies and improving efficiency shift energy usage away from oil.</p>
<p>Worldwide oil discoveries have been less than annual production since 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-ewg1007_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewg1007-6">[6]</a></sup> World population has grown faster than oil production. Because of this, oil production <i><a href="/wiki/Per_capita" title="Per capita">per capita</a></i> peaked in 1979 (preceded by a plateau during the period of 1973–1979).<sup id="cite_ref-RCDuncan2001_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RCDuncan2001-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:552px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Countries_by_Oil_Production_in_2013.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Countries_by_Oil_Production_in_2013.svg/550px-Countries_by_Oil_Production_in_2013.svg.png" width="550" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Countries_by_Oil_Production_in_2013.svg/825px-Countries_by_Oil_Production_in_2013.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Countries_by_Oil_Production_in_2013.svg/1100px-Countries_by_Oil_Production_in_2013.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="863" data-file-height="396" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Countries_by_Oil_Production_in_2013.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Countries producing oil 2013, bbl/day (CIA World Factbook)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:552px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_producing_countries_map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Oil_producing_countries_map.png/550px-Oil_producing_countries_map.png" width="550" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Oil_producing_countries_map.png/825px-Oil_producing_countries_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Oil_producing_countries_map.png/1100px-Oil_producing_countries_map.png 2x" data-file-width="1427" data-file-height="628" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_producing_countries_map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Oil producing <a href="/wiki/List_of_oil-producing_states" class="mw-redirect" title="List of oil-producing states">countries</a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The increasing investment in harder-to-reach oil is a sign of oil companies' belief in the end of easy oil.<sup id="cite_ref-bostonglobe122005_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bostonglobe122005-62">[62]</a></sup> Also, while it is widely believed that increased oil prices spur an increase in production, an increasing number of oil industry insiders were reportedly coming to believe that even with higher prices, oil production was unlikely to increase significantly. Among the reasons cited were both geological factors as well as "above ground" factors that are likely to see oil production plateau.<sup id="cite_ref-reuters010908_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters010908-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>An important concept with regard to declining "easy oil" is <a href="/wiki/Energy_returned_on_energy_invested" title="Energy returned on energy invested">energy returned on energy invested</a>, also referred to as EROEI. A 2008 <i>Journal of Energy Security</i> analysis of the energy return on drilling effort in the United States concluded that there was extremely limited potential to increase production of both gas and (especially) oil. By looking at the historical response of production to variation in drilling effort, the analysis showed very little increase of production attributable to increased drilling. This was because of a tight quantitative relationship of diminishing returns with increasing drilling effort: as drilling effort increased, the energy obtained per active <a href="/wiki/Drill_rig" class="mw-redirect" title="Drill rig">drill rig</a> was reduced according to a severely diminishing <a href="/wiki/Power_law" title="Power law">power law</a>. The study concluded that even an enormous increase of drilling effort was unlikely to significantly increase oil and gas production in a mature petroleum region such as the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-TDKailing2008_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TDKailing2008-112">[112]</a></sup> However, contrary to the study's conclusion, since the analysis was published in 2008, US production of crude oil has increased 74%, and production of dry natural gas has increased 28% (2014 compared to 2008).<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anticipated_production_by_major_agencies">Anticipated production by major agencies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Anticipated production by major agencies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png/300px-2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png" width="300" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png/450px-2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png/600px-2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png 2x" data-file-width="753" data-file-height="586" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2012_Crude_Oil_Export_Treemap.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Crude oil export treemap (2012) from Harvard Atlas of Economic Complexity<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Average yearly gains in global supply from 1987 to 2005 were 1.2 million barrels per day (190<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>3</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d) (1.7%).<sup id="cite_ref-IEA:World_Oil_Supply_and_Demand_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA:World_Oil_Supply_and_Demand-115">[115]</a></sup> In 2005, the IEA predicted that 2030 production rates would reach 120,000,000 barrels per day (19,000,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d), but this number was gradually reduced to 105,000,000 barrels per day (16,700,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d). A 2008 analysis of IEA predictions questioned several underlying assumptions and claimed that a 2030 production level of 75,000,000 barrels per day (11,900,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d) (comprising 55,000,000 barrels (8,700,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of crude oil and 20,000,000 barrels (3,200,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of both non-conventional oil and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_liquids" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural gas liquids">natural gas liquids</a>) was more realistic than the IEA numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-alklett112009_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alklett112009-8">[8]</a></sup> More recently, the EIA's Annual Energy Outlook 2015 indicated no production peak out to 2040. However, this required a future Brent crude oil price of $US144/bbl (2013 dollars) "as growing demand leads to the development of more costly resources." <sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Whether the world economy can grow and maintain demand for such a high oil price remains to be seen.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_field_decline">Oil field decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Oil field decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
