<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alaska_Crude_Oil_Production.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Alaska_Crude_Oil_Production.PNG/220px-Alaska_Crude_Oil_Production.PNG" width="220" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Alaska_Crude_Oil_Production.PNG/330px-Alaska_Crude_Oil_Production.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Alaska_Crude_Oil_Production.PNG/440px-Alaska_Crude_Oil_Production.PNG 2x" data-file-width="668" data-file-height="650" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alaska_Crude_Oil_Production.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alaska's oil production has declined 70% since peaking in 1988</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In a 2013 study of 733 giant oil fields, only 32% of the ultimately recoverable oil, condensate and gas remained.<sup id="cite_ref-peakoilbarrel.com_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peakoilbarrel.com-117">[117]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ghawar" class="mw-redirect" title="Ghawar">Ghawar</a>, which is the largest oil field in the world and responsible for approximately half of Saudi Arabia's oil production over the last 50&#160;years, was in decline before 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> The world's second largest oil field, the <a href="/wiki/Burgan_Field" class="mw-redirect" title="Burgan Field">Burgan Field</a> in Kuwait, entered decline in November 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is well established that once an oilfield reaches maximum production, it will decrease at a certain decline rate. For example, Mexico announced that production from its giant <a href="/wiki/Cantarell_Field" title="Cantarell Field">Cantarell Field</a> began to decline in March 2006, reportedly at a rate of 13% per year.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Also in 2006, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Aramco" title="Saudi Aramco">Saudi Aramco</a> Senior Vice President Abdullah Saif estimated that its existing fields were declining at a rate of 5% to 12% per year.<sup id="cite_ref-eiaSAbrief_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eiaSAbrief-121">[121]</a></sup> According to a study of the largest 811 oilfields conducted in early 2008 by <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Energy_Research_Associates" title="Cambridge Energy Research Associates">Cambridge Energy Research Associates</a>, the average rate of field decline is 4.5% per year. The Association for the Study of Peak Oil and Gas agreed with their decline rates, but considered the rate of new fields coming online overly optimistic.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">IEA</a> stated in November 2008 that an analysis of 800 oilfields showed the decline in oil production to be 6.7% a year for fields past their peak, and that this would grow to 8.6% in 2030.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> A more rapid annual rate of decline of 5.1% in 800 of the world's largest oil fields weighted for production over their whole lives was reported by the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> in their <a href="/wiki/World_Energy_Outlook" title="World Energy Outlook">World Energy Outlook 2008</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iea-weo08_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-weo08-124">[124]</a></sup> The 2013 study of 733 giant fields mentioned previously had an average decline rate 3.83% which was described as "conservative."<sup id="cite_ref-peakoilbarrel.com_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peakoilbarrel.com-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Control_over_supply">Control over supply</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Control over supply">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Entities such as governments or cartels can reduce supply to the world market by limiting access to the supply through nationalizing oil, cutting back on production, limiting drilling rights, imposing taxes, etc. International sanctions, corruption, and military conflicts can also reduce supply.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nationalization_of_oil_supplies">Nationalization of oil supplies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Nationalization of oil supplies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nationalization_of_oil_supplies" title="Nationalization of oil supplies">Nationalization of oil supplies</a></div>
<p>Another factor affecting global oil supply is the <a href="/wiki/Nationalization" title="Nationalization">nationalization</a> of oil reserves by producing nations. The nationalization of oil occurs as countries begin to <a href="/wiki/Deprivatization" class="mw-redirect" title="Deprivatization">deprivatize</a> oil production and withhold exports. Kate Dourian, Platts' Middle East editor, points out that while estimates of oil reserves may vary, politics have now entered the equation of oil supply. "Some countries are becoming off limits. Major oil companies operating in Venezuela find themselves in a difficult position because of the growing nationalization of that resource. These countries are now reluctant to share their reserves."<sup id="cite_ref-arabianbusiness_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arabianbusiness-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to consulting firm <a href="/wiki/PFC_Energy" title="PFC Energy">PFC Energy</a>, only 7% of the world's estimated oil and gas reserves are in countries that allow companies like ExxonMobil free rein. Fully 65% are in the hands of state-owned companies such as Saudi Aramco, with the rest in countries such as Russia and Venezuela, where access by Western European and North American companies is difficult. The PFC study implies political factors are limiting capacity increases in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, Venezuela, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, and Russia. Saudi Arabia is also limiting capacity expansion, but because of a self-imposed cap, unlike the other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> As a result of not having access to countries amenable to oil exploration, ExxonMobil is not making nearly the investment in finding new oil that it did in 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="OPEC_influence_on_supply">OPEC influence on supply</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: OPEC influence on supply">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_the_Petroleum_Exporting_Countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries">Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:OPEC_Surplus_Crude_Capacity.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/OPEC_Surplus_Crude_Capacity.png/220px-OPEC_Surplus_Crude_Capacity.png" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/OPEC_Surplus_Crude_Capacity.png/330px-OPEC_Surplus_Crude_Capacity.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/OPEC_Surplus_Crude_Capacity.png/440px-OPEC_Surplus_Crude_Capacity.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="424" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:OPEC_Surplus_Crude_Capacity.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
OPEC surplus crude oil production capacity (US EIA)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>OPEC is an alliance between 12 diverse oil producing countries (Algeria, Angola, Ecuador, Iran, Iraq, Kuwait, Libya, Nigeria, Qatar, Saudi Arabia, the United Arab Emirates, and Venezuela) to control the supply of oil. OPEC's power was consolidated as various countries nationalized their oil holdings, and wrested decision-making away from the "<a href="/wiki/Seven_Sisters_(oil_companies)" title="Seven Sisters (oil companies)">Seven Sisters</a>," (Anglo-Iranian, Socony-Vacuum, Royal Dutch Shell, Gulf, Esso, Texaco, and Socal) and created their own oil companies to control the oil. OPEC tries to influence prices by restricting production. It does this by allocating each member country a quota for production. All 12 members agree to keep prices high by producing at lower levels than they otherwise would. There is no way to verify adherence to the quota, so every member faces the same incentive to "cheat" the cartel.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> United States policy of selling arms and providing security to Saudi Arabia is often seen as an attempt to influence the Saudis to increase oil production. According to sociology professor Michael Schwartz, the purpose for the second Iraq war was to break the back of OPEC and return control of the oil fields to western oil companies.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alternatively, commodities trader Raymond Learsy, author of <i>Over a Barrel: Breaking the Middle East Oil Cartel</i>, contends that OPEC has trained consumers to believe that oil is a much more finite resource than it is. To back his argument, he points to past false alarms and apparent collaboration.<sup id="cite_ref-nationalreview122003_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nationalreview122003-84">[84]</a></sup> He also believes that peak oil analysts are conspiring with OPEC and the oil companies to create a "fabricated drama of peak oil" to drive up oil prices and <a href="/wiki/Profit_(accounting)" title="Profit (accounting)">profits</a>; oil had risen to a little over $30/barrel at that time. A counter-argument was given in the Huffington Post after he and Steve Andrews, co-founder of ASPO, debated on CNBC in June 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Predictions_of_peak_oil">Predictions of peak oil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Predictions of peak oil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Predicting_the_timing_of_peak_oil" title="Predicting the timing of peak oil">Predicting the timing of peak oil</a></div>

<td colspan="6" style="font-size: 8pt; text-align:left">A selection of estimates of the year of peak world oil production, compiled by the United States Energy Information Administration</td>

</table>
<p>In 1962, Hubbert predicted that world oil production would peak at a rate of 12.5 billion barrels per year, around the year 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-M._King_Hubbert_1962_p.73-75_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M._King_Hubbert_1962_p.73-75-45">[45]</a></sup> In 1974, Hubbert predicted that peak oil would occur in 1995 "if current trends continue."<sup id="cite_ref-grove1974_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove1974-132">[132]</a></sup> Those predictions proved incorrect. However, a number of industry leaders and analysts believe that world oil production will peak between 2015 and 2030, with a significant chance that the peak will occur before 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> They consider dates after 2030 implausible.<sup id="cite_ref-madureira_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-madureira-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> By comparison, a 2014 analysis of production and reserve data predicted a peak in oil production about 2035.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Determining a more specific range is difficult due to the lack of certainty over the actual size of world oil reserves.<sup id="cite_ref-chapman_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chapman-137">[137]</a></sup> Unconventional oil is not currently predicted to meet the expected shortfall even in a best-case scenario.<sup id="cite_ref-madureira_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-madureira-134">[134]</a></sup> For unconventional oil to fill the gap without "potentially serious impacts on the global economy", oil production would have to remain stable after its peak, until 2035 at the earliest.<sup id="cite_ref-royal_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-royal-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>Papers published since 2010 have been relatively pessimistic. A 2010 <a href="/wiki/Kuwait_University" title="Kuwait University">Kuwait University</a> study predicted production would peak in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-pubs.acs.org_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pubs.acs.org-139">[139]</a></sup> A 2010 <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a> study predicted that production will peak before 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-owen032010_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-owen032010-10">[10]</a></sup> but its projection of a change soon "...&#160;from a demand-led market to a supply constrained market&#160;..." was incorrect. A 2014 validation of a significant 2004 study in the journal <i>Energy</i> proposed that it is likely that conventional oil production peaked, according to various definitions, between 2005 and 2011. A set of models published in a 2014 Ph.D. thesis predicted that a 2012 peak would be followed by a drop in oil prices, which in some scenarios could turn into a rapid rise in prices thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> According to energy blogger Ron Patterson, the peak of world oil production was probably around 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Major oil companies hit peak production in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Several sources in 2006 and 2007 predicted that worldwide production was at or past its maximum.<sup id="cite_ref-deffeyes012007_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deffeyes012007-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ewg1007_6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewg1007-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cohen102007_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cohen102007-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-koppelaar092006_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-koppelaar092006-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fatih_Birol" title="Fatih Birol">Fatih Birol</a>, chief economist at the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a>, also stated that "crude oil production for the world has already peaked in 2006."<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> However, in 2013 OPEC's figures showed that world crude oil production and remaining proven reserves were at record highs.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Simmons" title="Matthew Simmons">Matthew Simmons</a>, former Chairman of <a href="/wiki/Simmons_%26_Company_International" title="Simmons &amp; Company International">Simmons &amp; Company International</a> and author of <i>Twilight in the Desert: The Coming Saudi Oil Shock and the World Economy</i>, "peaking is one of these fuzzy events that you only know clearly when you see it through a rear view mirror, and by then an alternate resolution is generally too late."<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_consequences">Possible consequences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Possible consequences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hirsch_Report" class="mw-redirect" title="Hirsch Report">Hirsch Report</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malthusian_catastrophe" title="Malthusian catastrophe">Malthusian catastrophe</a></div>
<p>The wide use of fossil fuels has been one of the most important stimuli of <a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">economic growth</a> and prosperity since the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a>, allowing humans to participate in takedown, or the consumption of energy at a greater rate than it is being replaced. Some believe that when oil production decreases, human culture and modern technological society will be forced to change drastically. The impact of peak oil will depend heavily on the rate of decline and the development and adoption of <a href="/wiki/Mitigation_of_peak_oil" title="Mitigation of peak oil">effective alternatives</a>.</p>
<p>In 2005, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">United States Department of Energy</a> published a report titled <i>Peaking of World Oil Production: Impacts, Mitigation, &amp; Risk Management</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-hirsch_report_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hirsch_report-146">[146]</a></sup> Known as the <a href="/wiki/Hirsch_report" title="Hirsch report">Hirsch report</a>, it stated, "The peaking of world oil production presents the U.S. and the world with an unprecedented risk management problem. As peaking is approached, liquid fuel prices and price volatility will increase dramatically, and, without timely mitigation, the economic, social, and political costs will be unprecedented. Viable mitigation options exist on both the supply and demand sides, but to have substantial impact, they must be initiated more than a decade in advance of peaking." Some of the information was updated in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_prices">Oil prices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Oil prices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/World_oil_market_chronology_from_2003" title="World oil market chronology from 2003">World oil market chronology from 2003</a> and <a href="/wiki/Price_of_petroleum" class="mw-redirect" title="Price of petroleum">Price of petroleum</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_oil_prices">Historical oil prices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Historical oil prices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_Prices_Since_1861.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Oil_Prices_Since_1861.svg/400px-Oil_Prices_Since_1861.svg.png" width="400" height="114" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Oil_Prices_Since_1861.svg/600px-Oil_Prices_Since_1861.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Oil_Prices_Since_1861.svg/800px-Oil_Prices_Since_1861.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1450" data-file-height="414" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_Prices_Since_1861.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Long-term oil prices, 1861–2015 (top line adjusted for inflation)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The oil price historically was comparatively low until the <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil crisis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/1979_energy_crisis" title="1979 energy crisis">1979 energy crisis</a> when it increased more than tenfold during that six-year timeframe. Even though the oil price dropped significantly in the following years, it has never come back to the previous levels. Oil price began to increase again during the 2000s until it hit historical heights of $143 per barrel (2007 inflation adjusted dollars) on 30 June 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> As these prices were well above those that caused <a href="/wiki/1973_energy_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="1973 energy crisis">the 1973</a> and <a href="/wiki/1979_energy_crisis" title="1979 energy crisis">1979 energy crises</a>, they contributed to fears of an economic recession similar to that of the early 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-bruno032008_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bruno032008-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is generally agreed that the main reason for the price spike in 2005–2008 was strong demand pressure.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> For example, global consumption of oil rose from 30 billion barrels (4.8<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>9</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) in 2004 to 31 billion in 2005. The consumption rates were far above new discoveries in the period, which had fallen to only eight billion barrels of new oil reserves in new accumulations in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_company_asset_write-downs_2015.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Oil_company_asset_write-downs_2015.jpg/220px-Oil_company_asset_write-downs_2015.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Oil_company_asset_write-downs_2015.jpg/330px-Oil_company_asset_write-downs_2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Oil_company_asset_write-downs_2015.jpg/440px-Oil_company_asset_write-downs_2015.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="467" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_company_asset_write-downs_2015.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Asset write downs for oil companies 2015</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Oil price increases were partially fueled by reports that petroleum production is at<sup id="cite_ref-deffeyes012007_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deffeyes012007-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ewg1007_6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewg1007-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cohen102007_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cohen102007-7">[7]</a></sup> or near full capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-koppelaar092006_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-koppelaar092006-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wsj111907golddavis_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj111907golddavis-152">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-153">[153]</a></sup> In June 2005, OPEC stated that they would 'struggle' to pump enough oil to meet pricing pressures for the fourth quarter of that year.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> From 2007 to 2008, the decline in the U.S. dollar against other significant currencies was also considered as a significant reason for the oil price increases,<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> as the dollar lost approximately 14% of its value against the Euro from May 2007 to May 2008.</p>
<p>Besides supply and demand pressures, at times security related factors may have contributed to increases in prices,<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-153">[153]</a></sup> including the <a href="/wiki/War_on_Terror" title="War on Terror">War on Terror</a>, missile launches in <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/2006_Israel-Lebanon_conflict" class="mw-redirect" title="2006 Israel-Lebanon conflict">Crisis between Israel and Lebanon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> nuclear <a href="/wiki/Brinkmanship" title="Brinkmanship">brinkmanship</a> between the U.S. and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> and reports from the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Energy" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of Energy">U.S. Department of Energy</a> and others showing a decline in <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_reserves" class="mw-redirect" title="Petroleum reserves">petroleum reserves</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WTI_EIA_projections_2016-17.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/WTI_EIA_projections_2016-17.png/220px-WTI_EIA_projections_2016-17.png" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/WTI_EIA_projections_2016-17.png/330px-WTI_EIA_projections_2016-17.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/WTI_EIA_projections_2016-17.png/440px-WTI_EIA_projections_2016-17.png 2x" data-file-width="539" data-file-height="393" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WTI_EIA_projections_2016-17.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Depicts EIA projections for West Texas Intermediate crude oil price for 2016-2017</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>More recently, between 2011 and 2014 the price of crude oil was relatively stable, fluctuating around $US100 per barrel. It dropped sharply in late 2014 to below $US70 where it remained for most of 2015. In early 2016 it traded at a low of $US27.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> The price drop has been attributed to both oversupply and reduced demand as a result of the slowing global economy,<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> OPEC reluctance to concede market share,<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> and a stronger US dollar.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> These factors may be exacerbated by a combination of monetary policy and the increased debt of oil producers, who may increase production to maintain liquidity.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>This price drop has placed many US tight oil producers under considerable financial pressure.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> As a result, there has been a reduction by oil companies in capital expenditure of over $US400 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> It is anticipated that this will have effects on global production in the longer term, leading to statements of concern by the International Energy Agency that governments should not be complacent about energy security.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Energy Information Agency projections anticipate market oversupply and prices below $US50 until late 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_historical_oil_price_rises">Effects of historical oil price rises</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Effects of historical oil price rises">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Oil_price_increases_of_2004-2008#Effects" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil price increases of 2004-2008">Effects of oil price</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a href="/wiki/File:World_energy_consumption.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/World_energy_consumption.svg/270px-World_energy_consumption.svg.png" width="270" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/World_energy_consumption.svg/405px-World_energy_consumption.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/World_energy_consumption.svg/540px-World_energy_consumption.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="520" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:World_energy_consumption.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
World consumption of primary energy by energy type<sup id="cite_ref-BP-Review-2012_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BP-Review-2012-169">[169]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the past, the price of oil has led to economic <a href="/wiki/Recession" title="Recession">recessions</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/1973_energy_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="1973 energy crisis">1973</a> and <a href="/wiki/1979_energy_crisis" title="1979 energy crisis">1979 energy crises</a>. The effect the price of oil has on an economy is known as a <a href="/wiki/Price_shock" class="mw-redirect" title="Price shock">price shock</a>. In many European countries, which have <a href="/wiki/Gasoline_usage_and_pricing" class="mw-redirect" title="Gasoline usage and pricing">high taxes on fuels</a>, such price shocks could potentially be mitigated somewhat by temporarily or permanently suspending the taxes as fuel costs rise.<sup id="cite_ref-kanter112007_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kanter112007-170">[170]</a></sup> This method of softening price shocks is less useful in countries with much lower gas taxes, such as the United States. A baseline scenario for a recent <a href="/wiki/IMF" class="mw-redirect" title="IMF">IMF</a> paper found oil production growing at 0.8% (as opposed to a historical average of 1.8%) would result in a small reduction in economic growth of 0.2–0.4%.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>Researchers at the <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Energy_Modeling_Forum" class="mw-redirect" title="Stanford Energy Modeling Forum">Stanford Energy Modeling Forum</a> found that the economy can adjust to steady, gradual increases in the price of crude better than wild lurches.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some economists predict that a <a href="/wiki/Substitution_effect" title="Substitution effect">substitution effect</a> will spur demand for <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel" title="Alternative fuel">alternate energy sources</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_natural_gas" title="Liquefied natural gas">liquefied natural gas</a>. This substitution can be only temporary, as coal and natural gas are finite resources as well.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>Prior to the run-up in fuel prices, many motorists opted for larger, less fuel-efficient <a href="/wiki/Sport_utility_vehicles" class="mw-redirect" title="Sport utility vehicles">sport utility vehicles</a> and full-sized pickups in the United States, Canada, and other countries. This trend has been reversing because of sustained high prices of fuel. The September 2005 sales data for all vehicle vendors indicated SUV sales dropped while small cars sales increased. <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicles" class="mw-redirect" title="Hybrid vehicles">Hybrid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine" title="Diesel engine">diesel</a> vehicles are also gaining in popularity.<sup id="cite_ref-Fildes2007_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fildes2007-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>EIA published Household Vehicles Energy Use: Latest Data and Trends<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> in Nov 2005 illustrating the steady increase in disposable income and $20–30 per barrel price of oil in 2004. The report notes "The average household spent $1,520 on fuel purchases for transport." According to CNBC that expense climbed to $4,155 in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2008, a report by <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Energy_Research_Associates" title="Cambridge Energy Research Associates">Cambridge Energy Research Associates</a> stated that 2007 had been the year of peak gasoline usage in the United States, and that record energy prices would cause an "enduring shift" in energy consumption practices.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> The total miles driven in the U.S. peaked in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Export_Land_Model" title="Export Land Model">Export Land Model</a> states that after peak oil petroleum exporting countries will be forced to reduce their exports more quickly than their production decreases because of internal demand growth. Countries that rely on imported petroleum will therefore be affected earlier and more dramatically than exporting countries.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> Mexico is already in this situation. Internal consumption grew by 5.9% in 2006 in the five biggest exporting countries, and their exports declined by over 3%. It was estimated that by 2010 internal demand would decrease worldwide exports by 2,500,000 barrels per day (400,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d).<sup id="cite_ref-krauss1207_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krauss1207-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>Canadian economist Jeff Rubin has stated that high oil prices are likely to result in increased consumption in developed countries through partial manufacturing de-globalisation of trade. Manufacturing production would move closer to the end consumer to minimise transportation network costs, and therefore a demand decoupling from gross domestic product would occur. Higher oil prices would lead to increased freighting costs and consequently, the manufacturing industry would move back to the developed countries since freight costs would outweigh the current economic wage advantage of developing countries.<sup id="cite_ref-manufacturing-relocalisation_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-manufacturing-relocalisation-181">[181]</a></sup> Economic research carried out by the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> puts overall <a href="/wiki/Price_elasticity_of_demand" title="Price elasticity of demand">price elasticity of demand</a> for oil at −0.025 short-term and −0.093 long term.<sup id="cite_ref-imf-demand-elasticities_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-imf-demand-elasticities-182">[182]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_effects_and_population_limits">Agricultural effects and population limits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Agricultural effects and population limits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Food_vs_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Food vs fuel">Food vs fuel</a>, <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%932008_world_food_price_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2007–2008 world food price crisis">2007–2008 world food price crisis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agriculture#Energy_and_agriculture" title="Agriculture">Agriculture §&#160;Energy and agriculture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">Food security</a></div>
<p>Since supplies of oil and gas are essential to <a href="/wiki/Intensive_agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="Intensive agriculture">modern agriculture</a> techniques, a fall in global oil supplies could cause spiking food prices and unprecedented <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a> in the coming decades.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[note 1]</a></sup> Geologist <a href="/wiki/Dale_Allen_Pfeiffer" title="Dale Allen Pfeiffer">Dale Allen Pfeiffer</a> contends that current population levels are unsustainable, and that to achieve a sustainable economy and avert <a href="/wiki/Disaster" title="Disaster">disaster</a> the United States population would have to be reduced by at least one-third, and world population by two-thirds.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>The largest consumer of fossil fuels in modern agriculture is <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_production" title="Ammonia production">ammonia production</a> (for <a href="/wiki/Mineral_fertilizer" class="mw-redirect" title="Mineral fertilizer">fertilizer</a>) via the <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">Haber process</a>, which is essential to high-yielding <a href="/wiki/Intensive_agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="Intensive agriculture">intensive agriculture</a>. The specific fossil fuel input to fertilizer production is primarily <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, to provide <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> via <a href="/wiki/Steam_reforming" title="Steam reforming">steam reforming</a>. Given sufficient supplies of <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable electricity</a>, hydrogen can be generated without fossil fuels using methods such as <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water#Applications" title="Electrolysis of water">electrolysis</a>. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Vemork" title="Vemork">Vemork</a> hydroelectric plant in Norway used its surplus electricity output to generate <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_production#Sustainable_ammonia_production" title="Ammonia production">renewable ammonia</a> from 1911 to 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-bradley_2004_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bradley_2004-187">[186]</a></sup></p>
<p>Iceland currently generates ammonia using the electrical output from its <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_Iceland" title="Renewable energy in Iceland">hydroelectric and geothermal power plants</a>, because Iceland has those resources in abundance while having no domestic hydrocarbon resources, and a high cost for importing natural gas.<sup id="cite_ref-iceland_ammonia_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iceland_ammonia-188">[187]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Long-term_effects_on_lifestyle">Long-term effects on lifestyle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Long-term effects on lifestyle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EIA_global_transport_energy_by_fuel_2012.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/EIA_global_transport_energy_by_fuel_2012.png/220px-EIA_global_transport_energy_by_fuel_2012.png" width="220" height="119" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/EIA_global_transport_energy_by_fuel_2012.png/330px-EIA_global_transport_energy_by_fuel_2012.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/EIA_global_transport_energy_by_fuel_2012.png/440px-EIA_global_transport_energy_by_fuel_2012.png 2x" data-file-width="499" data-file-height="271" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EIA_global_transport_energy_by_fuel_2012.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
World transport energy use by fuel type 2012</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Peak_car" title="Peak car">Peak car</a></div>
<p>A majority of Americans live in <a href="/wiki/Suburbs" class="mw-redirect" title="Suburbs">suburbs</a>, a type of low-density settlement designed around universal personal <a href="/wiki/Automobile" class="mw-redirect" title="Automobile">automobile</a> use. Commentators such as <a href="/wiki/James_Howard_Kunstler" title="James Howard Kunstler">James Howard Kunstler</a> argue that because over 90% of transportation in the U.S. relies on oil, the suburbs' reliance on the automobile is an unsustainable living arrangement. Peak oil would leave many Americans unable to afford petroleum based fuel for their cars, and force them to use <a href="/wiki/Bicycles" class="mw-redirect" title="Bicycles">bicycles</a> or <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicles" class="mw-redirect" title="Electric vehicles">electric vehicles</a>. Additional options include <a href="/wiki/Telecommuting" title="Telecommuting">telecommuting</a>, moving to <a href="/wiki/Rural_areas" class="mw-redirect" title="Rural areas">rural areas</a>, or moving to higher density areas, where <a href="/wiki/Walking" title="Walking">walking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Public_transportation" class="mw-redirect" title="Public transportation">public transportation</a> are more viable options. In the latter two cases, suburbs may become the "<a href="/wiki/Slum" title="Slum">slums</a> of the future."<sup id="cite_ref-geonowhere_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geonowhere-189">[188]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[189]</a></sup> The issue of petroleum supply and demand is also a concern for growing cities in developing countries (where urban areas are expected to absorb most of the world's projected 2.3 billion population increase by 2050). Stressing the energy component of future development plans is seen as an important goal.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[190]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rising oil prices, if they occur, would also affect the cost of food, heating, and electricity. A high amount of stress would then be put on current middle to low income families as economies contract from the decline in excess funds, decreasing employment rates. The Hirsch/US DoE Report concludes that "without timely mitigation, world supply/demand balance will be achieved through massive demand destruction (shortages), accompanied by huge oil price increases, both of which would create a long period of significant economic hardship worldwide."<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>Methods that have been suggested for mitigating these urban and suburban issues include the use of non-petroleum vehicles such as <a href="/wiki/Electric_car" title="Electric car">electric cars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battery_electric_vehicle" title="Battery electric vehicle">battery electric vehicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transit-oriented_development" title="Transit-oriented development">transit-oriented development</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carfree_city" title="Carfree city">carfree cities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bicycle" title="Bicycle">bicycles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Light_rail" title="Light rail">new trains</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_pedestrianism" title="New pedestrianism">new pedestrianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smart_growth" title="Smart growth">smart growth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shared_space" title="Shared space">shared space</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urban_consolidation" title="Urban consolidation">urban consolidation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urban_village" title="Urban village">urban villages</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_Urbanism" title="New Urbanism">New Urbanism</a>.</p>
<p>An extensive 2009 report on the effects of compact development by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Research Council">United States National Research Council</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academy_of_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Academy of Sciences">Academy of Sciences</a>, commissioned by the United States Congress, stated six main findings.<sup id="cite_ref-Driving_the_Built_Environment_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Driving_the_Built_Environment-193">[192]</a></sup> First, that compact development is likely to reduce "Vehicle Miles Traveled" (VMT) throughout the country. Second, that doubling residential density in a given area could reduce VMT by as much as 25% if coupled with measures such as increased employment density and improved public transportation. Third, that higher density, mixed-use developments would produce both direct reductions in CO<sub>2</sub> emissions (from less driving), and indirect reductions (such as from lower amounts of materials used per housing unit, higher efficiency climate control, longer vehicle lifespans, and higher efficiency delivery of goods and services). Fourth, that although short term reductions in energy use and CO<sub>2</sub> emissions would be modest, that these reductions would become more significant over time. Fifth, that a major obstacle to more compact development in the United States is political resistance from local zoning regulators, which would hamper efforts by state and regional governments to participate in land-use planning. Sixth, the committee agreed that changes in development that would alter driving patterns and building efficiency would have various secondary costs and benefits that are difficult to quantify. The report recommends that policies supporting compact development (and especially its ability to reduce driving, energy use, and CO<sub>2</sub> emissions) should be encouraged.</p>
<p>An economic theory that has been proposed as a remedy is the introduction of a <a href="/wiki/Steady_state_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Steady state economy">steady state economy</a>. Such a system could include a tax shifting from income to depleting natural resources (and pollution), as well as the limitation of advertising that stimulates demand and population growth. It could also include the institution of policies that move away from globalization and toward localization to conserve energy resources, provide local jobs, and maintain local decision-making authority. Zoning policies could be adjusted to promote resource conservation and eliminate sprawl.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[193]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[194]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since aviation relies mainly on <a href="/wiki/Jet_fuel" title="Jet fuel">jet fuels</a> derived from crude oil, <a href="/wiki/Commercial_aviation" title="Commercial aviation">commercial aviation</a> has been predicted to go into decline with the global oil production.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[195]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mitigation">Mitigation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Mitigation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mitigation_of_peak_oil" title="Mitigation of peak oil">Mitigation of peak oil</a></div>
<p>To avoid the serious <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">social</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economic</a> implications a global decline in oil production could entail, the <a href="/wiki/Hirsch_report" title="Hirsch report">Hirsch report</a> emphasized the need to find alternatives, at least ten to twenty years before the peak, and to phase out the use of petroleum over that time.<sup id="cite_ref-hirsch_report_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hirsch_report-146">[146]</a></sup> This was similar to <a href="/wiki/Oil_phase-out_in_Sweden" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil phase-out in Sweden">a plan proposed for Sweden</a> that same year. Such <a href="/wiki/Mitigation_of_peak_oil" title="Mitigation of peak oil">mitigation</a> could include energy conservation, fuel substitution, and the use of unconventional oil. The timing of mitigation responses is critical. Premature initiation would be undesirable, but if initiated too late could be more costly and have more negative economic consequences.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[196]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Positive_aspects">Positive aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Positive aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Permaculture" title="Permaculture">Permaculture</a> sees peak oil as holding tremendous potential for positive change, assuming countries act with foresight. The rebuilding of local food networks, energy production, and the general implementation of "<a href="/wiki/Energy_descent" title="Energy descent">energy descent</a> culture" are argued to be ethical responses to the acknowledgment of finite fossil resources.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[197]</a></sup> Majorca is an island currently diversifying its energy supply from fossil fuels to alternative sources and looking back at traditional construction and permaculture methods.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Transition_Towns" class="mw-redirect" title="Transition Towns">Transition Towns</a> movement, started in <a href="/wiki/Totnes" title="Totnes">Totnes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[199]</a></sup> and spread internationally by "The Transition Handbook" (<a href="/wiki/Rob_Hopkins" title="Rob Hopkins">Rob Hopkins</a>) and Transition Network, sees the restructuring of society for more local resilience and ecological stewardship as a natural response to the combination of peak oil and climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[200]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticisms">Criticisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Criticisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_arguments">General arguments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: General arguments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Opponents to the theory of peak oil often cite new oil reserves that have been found, which continue to forestall a peak oil event. In particular, some contend that oil production from these new oil reserves as well as from existing fields will continue to increase at a rate that outpaces demand, until alternate energy sources for our current <a href="/wiki/Energy_dependence" class="mw-redirect" title="Energy dependence">fossil fuel dependence</a> are found.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[201]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stanford_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford-203">[202]</a></sup> As of 2015, analysts in both the petroleum and financial industries were concluding that the "age of oil" had already reached a new stage where the excess supply that appeared in late 2014 may continue to prevail in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-Spencer_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spencer-204">[203]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shilling_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shilling-205">[204]</a></sup> A consensus appeared to be emerging that an international agreement will be reached to introduce measures to constrain the combustion of hydrocarbons in an effort to limit global temperature rise to the nominal 2&#160;°C that is consensually predicted to limit environmental harm to tolerable levels.<sup id="cite_ref-Paris_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paris-206">[205]</a></sup></p>
<p>Further criticism against peak oil is confidence in the various options and technologies for <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel" title="Alternative fuel">substituting oil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[206]</a></sup> And indeed there are some promising approaches that seem to have the potential to reduce or even counterbalance the effects of a peak oil situation. For example, US federal funding has increased for <a href="/wiki/Algal_fuel" class="mw-redirect" title="Algal fuel">algae fuels</a> since the year 2000 due to rising fuel prices.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[207]</a></sup> Numerous more projects are being funded in Australia, New Zealand, Europe, the Middle East, and other parts of the world<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[208]</a></sup> and private companies are entering the field.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[209]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_industry_representatives">Oil industry representatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Oil industry representatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Oil industry representatives have criticised peak oil theory, at least as it has been presented by <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Simmons" title="Matthew Simmons">Matthew Simmons</a>. The president of <a href="/wiki/Royal_Dutch_Shell" title="Royal Dutch Shell">Royal Dutch Shell</a>'s U.S. operations John Hofmeister, while agreeing that conventional oil production would soon start to decline, criticized Simmons's analysis for being "overly focused on a single country: Saudi Arabia, the world's largest exporter and OPEC <a href="/wiki/Swing_producer" title="Swing producer">swing producer</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-cnbc.com_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnbc.com-211">[210]</a></sup> He also pointed to the large reserves at the US <a href="/wiki/Outer_continental_shelf" class="mw-redirect" title="Outer continental shelf">outer continental shelf</a>, which held an estimated 100 billion barrels (16<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>9</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of oil and natural gas. However, only 15% of those reserves were currently exploitable, a good part of that off the coasts of Louisiana, Alabama, Mississippi, and Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-cnbc.com_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnbc.com-211">[210]</a></sup> Hofmeister also contended that Simmons erred in excluding unconventional sources of oil such as the oil sands of Canada, where Shell was active. The Canadian oil sands—a natural combination of sand, water, and oil found largely in Alberta and Saskatchewan—are believed to contain one trillion barrels of oil. Another trillion barrels are also said to be trapped in rocks in Colorado, Utah, and Wyoming,<sup id="cite_ref-HofmeisterPeakOilCriticism_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HofmeisterPeakOilCriticism-212">[211]</a></sup> but are in the form of <a href="/wiki/Oil_shale" title="Oil shale">oil shale</a>. These particular reserves present major environmental, social, and economic obstacles to recovery.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[212]</a></sup> Hofmeister also claimed that if oil companies were allowed to drill more in the United States enough to produce another 2 million barrels per day (320<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>3</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d), oil and gas prices would not be as high as they were in the later part of the 2000 to 2010 decade. He thought in 2008 that high energy prices would cause social unrest similar to the <a href="/wiki/1992_Los_Angeles_riots" title="1992 Los Angeles riots">1992 Rodney King riots</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HofmeiserCharlieRose_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HofmeiserCharlieRose-214">[213]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009, Dr. Christoph Rühl, chief economist of <a href="/wiki/BP" title="BP">BP</a>, argued against the peak oil hypothesis:<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[214]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>Physical peak oil, which I have no reason to accept as a valid statement either on theoretical, scientific or ideological grounds, would be insensitive to prices.&#160;... In fact the whole hypothesis of peak oil – which is that there is a certain amount of oil in the ground, consumed at a certain rate, and then it's finished – does not react to anything&#160;... Therefore there will never be a moment when the world runs out of oil because there will always be a price at which the last drop of oil can clear the market. And you can turn anything into oil into if you are willing to pay the financial and environmental price&#160;... (Global Warming) is likely to be more of a natural limit than all these peak oil theories combined.&#160;... Peak oil has been predicted for 150 years. It has never happened, and it will stay this way.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>According to Rühl, the main limitations for oil availability are "above ground" and are to be found in the availability of staff, expertise, technology, investment security, money and last but not least in global warming. The oil question is about price and not the basic availability. Rühl's views are shared by <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Yergin" title="Daniel Yergin">Daniel Yergin</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Energy_Research_Associates" title="Cambridge Energy Research Associates">CERA</a>, who added that the recent high price phase might add to a future demise of the oil industry, not of complete exhaustion of resources or an apocalyptic shock but the timely and smooth setup of alternatives.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[215]</a></sup></p>
<p>Clive Mather, CEO of Shell Canada, said the Earth's supply of <a href="/wiki/Bitumen" class="mw-redirect" title="Bitumen">bitumen</a> hydrocarbons is "almost infinite", referring to hydrocarbons in <a href="/wiki/Oil_sands" title="Oil sands">oil sands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shell_Canda_CEO_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shell_Canda_CEO-217">[216]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Others">Others</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peak_oil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Others">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Economist <a href="/wiki/Robert_L._Bradley,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Robert L. Bradley, Jr.">Robert L. Bradley, Jr.</a> wrote in a 2007 article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Review_of_Austrian_Economics" title="The Review of Austrian Economics">The Review of Austrian Economics</a></i> that, "[a]n <a href="/wiki/Austrian_School" title="Austrian School">Austrian</a> institutional theory is more robust for explaining changes in mineral-resource scarcity than neoclassical depletionism[.]"<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[217]</a></sup> Using the writings of <a href="/wiki/Erich_Zimmermann" title="Erich Zimmermann">Erich Zimmermann</a> and Julian Simon, Bradley also argued in 2012 that resources have <a href="/wiki/Subjective_theory_of_value" title="Subjective theory of value">subjective</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Objectivity_(philosophy)" title="Objectivity (philosophy)">objective</a> existences in economics. He concluded that, "what resources come from the ground ultimately depend on the resources in the mind."<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[218]</a></sup></p>
<p>Attorney and mechanical engineer <a href="/wiki/Peter_W._Huber" title="Peter W. Huber">Peter W. Huber</a> pointed out in 2006 that the world is just running out of "cheap oil." As oil prices rise, unconventional sources become economically viable. He predicted that, "[t]he tar sands of Alberta alone contain enough hydrocarbon to fuel the entire planet for over 100 years."<sup id="cite_ref-Shell_Canda_CEO_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shell_Canda_CEO-217">[216]</a></sup></p>
<p>Industry blogger Steve Maley echoed some of the points of Yergin, Rühl, Mather and Hofmeister.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[219]</a></sup></p>
<p>Environmental journalist <a href="/wiki/George_Monbiot" title="George Monbiot">George Monbiot</a> responded to a 2012 report by <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Maugeri" title="Leonardo Maugeri">Leonardo Maugeri</a><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[220]</a></sup> by proclaiming that there is more than enough oil (from unconventional sources) for capitalism to "deep-fry" the world with climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-peakoil_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peakoil-222">[221]</a></sup> Stephen Sorrell, senior lecturer <a href="/wiki/Science_and_Technology_Policy_Research" class="mw-redirect" title="Science and Technology Policy Research">Science and Technology Policy Research</a>, Sussex Energy Group, and lead author of the <a href="/wiki/UK_Energy_Research_Centre" title="UK Energy Research Centre">UKERC</a> Global Oil Depletion report, and Christophe McGlade, doctoral researcher at the UCL Energy Institute have criticized Maugeri's assumptions about decline rates.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[222]</a></sup></p>
